1400069289|5.0|Following a prologue in which a subject transforms utterly transforms herself, Duhigg lays out the structure of the book.  \\"Part One: The Habits of Individuals\\" is broken into three chapters.<br /><br />Chapter 1, \\"The Habit Loop\\" describes the (wait for it...) the habit loop, which is the foundation for everything that follows.  This is a 3-step process, in which a cue triggers a routine which is reinforced by a reward.  Duhigg does a great job of describing the science that describes this pattern, and the science which explains it, without making the information so dry that you can't absorb it.<br /><br />Chapter 2, \\"The Craving Brain,\\" examines individuals who suffered neurological damage and the impact that habits had on their ability to perform various functions and routines.  This chapter had heart: imagining the daily lives of these individuals and their caregivers brought some real drama to the study of how habits operate in our brains.  The point of the chapter was basically that habits are surprisingly delicate, to use Duhigg's term, and can be easily disrupted, with the right information.<br /><br />Chapter 3, \\"The Golden Rule of Habit Change: Why Transformation Occurs\\" focused on the coaching career of of NFL coach Tony Dungy, and how he used his understanding of habits to transform the Tampa Bay Buccaneers and the Indiana Colts.  The Golden Rule is You can't extinguish a bad habit, you can only change it.  Midway through Chapter 3 Duhigg breaks away from NFL to consider Alcoholics Anonymous.  If Chapter 2 is the heart of Part One, then Chapter 3 is the soul: both AA and Dungy's football program achieve their greatest success when the people operating under their respective guidance both arrive at belief in something greater than the individual.  Duhigg shares more than that in this chapter, but there is a ton of information in this chapter about how habits can be disrupted to make way for more positive patterns.<br /><br />In Chapter 4, \\"Keystone Habits, Or the Ballad of Paul O'Neill: Which Habits Matter Most,\\" Duhigg offers Paul O'Neill of Alcoa to illustrate how altering a single habit in an organization (albeit in a highly focused and disciplined manner) can transform the total organization.<br /><br />Chapter 5, \\"Starbucks and the Habit of Success,\\" opens with a powerful story of a young man who was raised by drug addicts, and his subsequent struggles to maintain his employment.  His pattern of failure changed when he went to work at Starbucks.  This chapter discusses the importance of willpower and its limitations, how willpower can be strengthened, and planning for success.<br /><br />Chapter 6, \\"The Power of a Crisis,\\" uses the examples of doctor error in a Rhode Island hospital, which Duhigg asserts was made inevitable by the toxic atmosphere in the workplace, and a fire in King's Cross Station, London, which was made inevitable by strictly observed divisions of labor, to provide opportunities for transforming the cultures of those two organizations into something stronger and more effective than could have been created as Paul O'Neill did, just by sheer force of leadership.<br /><br />Chapter 7, \\"How Target Knows What You Want Before You Do\\" is probably the most widely read section of the book, as it was excerpted by the New York Times (Duhigg's employer) and Forbes, among others.  It's readable and informative, and fairly creepy in disclosing how much information we unwittingly distribute about ourselves, and how unlikely we are to curtail the activities that make it possible for Target to know a woman is pregnant before any of her immediate family members do.<br /><br />Several reviewers have described these sections as \\"filler,\\" but I found that they addressed complaints common to people who claim to want to change their habits but lack willpower, and provided guideposts to an attentive reader for what qualities set one up for success. I did not find these sections to be filler, but powerful illustrations of how a thorough understanding of the mechanisms behind habits can provide the tools for large scale change, and a discussion of the nature of personal responsibility.  Although the sections were more directly addressing corporate bodies, the information was driven by the individuals within those organizations and therefore applicable to me and my own private attempts to alter my habits.<br /><br />Part Three was an interesting summing up.  Chapter 8, \\"Saddleback Church and the Montgomery Bus Boycott\\" addressed the components that made those movements (if one can call a mega-church a movement) successful.  The Montgomery Bus Boycott, particularly, was really interesting: we celebrate Rosa Parks's heroism, deservedly, but the fact is, several other individuals had made similar stands without sparking the Civil Rights Movement.  Duhigg's explanation for why Parks had the right stuff to make it happen makes for informative reading (the short version being, Parks was a genuinely nice and widely connected member of Montgomery society).<br /><br />Chapter 9, \\"The Neurology of Free Will: Are We Responsible for Our Habits?\\" puts all the preceding information in perspective.  It contrasts Brian Thomas, an Englishman who killed his wife while sleepwalking, with Angie Bachmann, a compulsive gambler who lost many hundreds of thousands of dollars.  He describes the neurology of sleepwalking activity and of a compulsive activity such as gambling (or drinking, or binge eating) and concludes that habits are under are control and can be altered, which argues for self-awareness and personal responsibility.<br /><br />The information provided in the body of the book was enough for me to understand how to create a road map for how to change my habits, but Duhigg did provide a digest of the material in his Appendix, \\"A Reader's Guide to Using These Ideas.\\" Overall, I found this book to be both readable and powerful, and I look forward to implementing what I've learned to further my own goals.ons of how a thorough understanding of the mechanisms behind habits can provide the tools for large scale change, and a discussion of the nature of personal responsibility. B007I2BZIE|3.0|Let me first say that it does my heart good to see so many five-star reviews.  Rush is one of my favorites and I love seeing the support and the adulations.  I almost feel bad not adding another five-star review to the page, but I couldn't do that when I don't believe it.<br /><br />I think you have to look at Clockwork Angels in two different ways: musically and lyrically.  Musically I would give this album six stars out of five.  Truly this is evidence of three stellar musicians at the top of their game, with excellently mixed songs devoid of the almost forced clutter of Snakes and Arrow.  I even loved being able to pick out sound tropes here and there that made me say, \\"Wow, that sounds like it could have come from Signals/Caress of Steel/Test for Echo/pick a dang album; I wonder if that was intentional or a happy accident?\\"  Honestly this could have been a largely instrumental affair and I wouldn't have felt cheated in the slightest.<br /><br />Lyrically, though, is where I had problems with this album.  Overall I can't call myself a fan of \\"concept albums\\" in general -- it's a tricky thing to pull off and pull off well.  And technically Rush have never had a true concept album, just a few concept A-sides (\\"2112,\\" \\"Hemispheres\\") and less well-received concept B-sides (\\"The Necromancer\\" & \\"The Fountains of Lamneth\\").  I won't say there aren't fantastic songs on Clockwork Angels -- I think that when it works, it works exceedingly well.  BU2B, The Wreckers and Headlong Flight are all tremendous tracks both lyrically and musically.  The problem with many of the other songs is that they feel shoehorned into a concept that I don't think works very well to begin with; in fact, unless you read the liner notes and get the little snippets of storyline (I'm sure these will be expanded on in the novel) they don't particularly tell a story.  That's the issue -- a concept album tells a coherent story from beginning to end.  \\"2112\\" did this, as did \\"Hemispheres.\\"  Even the epics from Caress of Steel told full stories.  I just don't feel that Clockwork Angels nailed it in this respect.<br /><br />But, as a Rush fan, I'm glad to hear them busting out the heavy guns again and I eagerly await their next release.  Keep up the good works, fellas!
B007TBCTRK|4.0|I feel as if Marc Streitenfeld has been under the industry microscope ever since he became Ridley Scott's go-to composer. He has never done a score without Ridley being involved in some fashion. His only two non-Ridley directed films are Welcome To The Riley's and The Grey; both are produced by Ridley Scott. Never has the microscope been more focused than for Prometheus; undoubtedly the biggest score of his career so far. I never had any doubts, but some people did. Then word got out that Harry Gregson-Williams had to come in to help with additional music. Why is this a big deal for many? I have no idea. The result is an absolutely masterful effort on Streitenfeld's part only enhanced by the grandiose wonder Harry brought to the table.<br /><br />Prometheus' score is such a complex soundscape of different layers, tones and melodies that all blend into this mysteriously wonderful terror. It's clear that Harry was brought in to give the film some big cosmic and existential theme, which is what he did. A theme that is referenced a few times throughout the film. It serves as an anchoring point but it blends so perfectly with what Streitenfeld did. Clearly Streitenfeld and Harry worked together on this one as you can hear this motif in the track \\"Earth\\", which is credited to Streitenfeld. So to say that Harry \\"rescued\\" the film is completely inaccurate. The other 99% of the score by Streitenfeld is dark, brooding and at times thrilling. The music builds on you and is never boring. The score is structured almost perfectly and in the film it plays such a major role as music always does in a Ridley Scott film. The subtle undertones work very well as do the big overt cues. The film is driven by the score and as a musically conscious viewer I felt this score working over me every step of the way. It builds such a solid universe and it feels brand new. Streitenfeld references Jerry Goldsmith's Alien theme ever so slightly in \\"Friend From The Past\\"; a perfect track title.<br /><br />Prometheus is a splendid wonder of brilliant scoring. It hits all the right beats and shows how talented the young Marc Streitenfeld is when it comes to hitting the right emotions. Harry's wondrous theme doesn't steal the show as some people may lead you to believe. It serves as a perfect accent to the already masterful score. Prometheus is a richly composed score that holds this great beast of a film together.<br />Prometheus is a splendid wonder of brilliant scoring. B00846REIS|4.0|-- A Romantic Book Affairs Review<br /><br />I'll start by addressing the elephant in the room - you know, that book everyone is talking about. Fifty Shades of Grey. Have I read it? Well, I read the first four or five chapters... Which isn't enough to give an opinion on the story itself, but was more than enough to form an opinion on the writing. And my opinion is decidedly negative. No matter how great a story it might be, with writing that bad... It's not for me.<br /><br />So while people will draw comparisons between Fifty and Bared to You, and there are quite a few similarities, Bared to You is infinitely and exponentially the more well written of the two.<br /><br />Now that that's off my chest... Let's forget about that other book and focus on Eva Tramell and Gideon Cross, shall we?<br /><br />I will admit that the first person narrative was jarring at first. It isn't terribly common in romance novels, as it limits the character perspective, and I know that some absolutely refuse to read a first person romance... but I thought it worked well with this story and I didn't find it to be a big deal at all. It certainly made Gideon all the more mysterious.<br /><br />And mysterious he is... and secretive and unreadable. After reading the book in its entirety, I still don't feel like I know who Gideon Cross is. I know that he's rich (an understatement, really) and devastatingly good-looking. He owns half of Manhattan and dresses impeccably. He's estranged from his family. He is riddled with deep emotional scars. And he likes to be in control at all times. I found Gideon to be quite the puzzle--albeit a very smexy one.<br /><br />As our narrator, Eva is much more transparent. All of her secrets are shared with us throughout... and each one revealed is quite the doozy. From the beginning, you know that Eva's life hasn't been the pretty picture it seems. However, it isn't until the middle of the book that you find out just how not pretty it really is. And it explained a lot about her. It's no surprise that Eva is easily jealous. That she's more flight than fight.<br /><br />Eva and Gideon's relationship can only be described as dysfunctional... but working on it. I loved it on PC and want to play it on a PS3 and later play Borderlands 2.<br /><br />I've tried a similar mouse and keyboard product and was not impressed. The precision was just not there and actually made playing worse. I was skeptical the Eagle Eye Converter would be any different. I was wrong. What makes the EEC different is the software that is used to configure the device before plugging into a console. Keys are mapped on a PC and then saved to the device. Up to 2 can be saved to the device at a time and as many as you need saved to your PC.<br /><br />You then plug the EEC into your console along with a mouse and keyboard (check online for a compatibility list). This is where the magic happens and where Eagle Eye stands apart! The device reserves the F1-F8 keys for fine tuning and saving options. F1 is used to eliminate dead zones, F2 for horizontal sensitivity, F3 for vertical, F4 Angle correction, F5 for rotation speed. I only used the first 3 as I found it to be perfect after adjusting those. Tip - turn the sensitivity up all the way in the game options and then back off with these settings for the most control.<br /><br />The EEC also features a really awesome feature where sensitivity settings are stored separately for scope/iron-sight. I configured the EEC to use different values when ever I hold down Right-Mouse. I didn't even know I needed this. Bravo!<br /><br />The instructions were very tricky to understand at first but if followed carefully, it is actually very easy to make these fine tuning adjustments.<br /><br />You can then connect to a PC again and save the final configuration values. Configurations can also be saved to Penguin United's website for others to use. Configurations are available to download for many popular games.<br /><br />Finally, I was able to play an FPS without wanting to throw my controller. No, it is not as good as a PC but it is really close. 9 out of 10 stars close. The number of keys available is limited to the number of buttons on a controller so keys will often play double duty. Also, navigating menus can be a little tricky because you have to memorize where you remapped all the buttons to on the keyboard.<br /><br />If the time is taken to fine tune it for each game, you will play just as well as you do on a PC. Mouse movements are translated well (even minor adjustments). Actions are very smooth. Well worth it.

1401937268|3.0|After a few months of fussing and fuming about the disingenuous promotions Hay House has used for the new Doreen Virtue Angel Tarot Cards, I am finally holding the deck in my hands. No, I didn't break down and make the purchase. If you are wondering why, see my blog post on the topic.<br /><br />However, I do believe the Doreen Virtue Angel Tarot is a legitimate contribution to the tarot corpus, and I am glad a friend lent me her copy so I could give it a legitimate review.<br /><br />Angel Tarot Cards (yes, that is the official title) looks like a typical Doreen Virtue oracle. It is oversized, printed on very sturdy stock and packaged in a lovely box with a small but substantial booklet. Because of their size and sturdiness, the cards are a bit hard to handle, but lovely to behold.<br /><br />The deck is a standard 78 card tarot deck in many respects. The artwork is gorgeous, but there are few visual clues in each picture about what the card might actually mean. Never fear, there are interpretations written on each card. There is no need to actually intuit or study anything. This is a boon for new tarot readers, or for those who want to use the deck as a simple, easy oracle.<br /><br />I like many of the written interpretations on the cards and in the booklet. Virtue and I agree that every tarot card can have an uplifting meaning. The punctuation on some of the interpretations summons my inner grammar police. The interpretations are a mishmash of full sentences and key words, but each thought is punctuated with a full stop. Apparently angels find no need for complete sentences or proper punctuation. We can't really blame the deck designers for this; many other oracle creators are guilty of the same lazy crime against the English language.<br /><br />The Major Arcana cards are each illustrated with an archangel. The cards bear the traditional number at the top. Each Major Arcana has a name, although many of the names have been changed from the traditional Major Arcana designations. Under the Major Arcana name is the name of the angel, then the illustration. The written interpretations are at the bottom.<br /><br />In this deck, Justice is card eight, and Strength is card eleven.  The Fool has been renamed as \\"The Dreamer.\\" Major Arcana five (Hierophant) is called \\"Unity,\\" twelve (Hanged Man) is called \\"Awakening,\\" thirteen (Death) is \\"Release,\\" fourteen (Temperance) is \\"Balance,\\" fifteen (Devil) is \\"Ego\\", sixteen (Tower) is \\"Life Experience\\" and twenty (Judgment) is \\"Renewal.\\" The rest retain their traditional names.<br /><br />I am comfortable with changing the traditional names of Major Arcana cards. In the five hundred year history of tarot some have already been changed. The Hierophant was once the Pope, for instance. I like Lisa Hunt's replacement of \\"Chains\\" for the Devil, and Eileen Connolly's \\"Transition\\" for Death.<br /><br />I think Virtue's \\"Release\\" for Death is brilliant. I also like \\"Ego\\" for the Devil. I think \\"Life Experience\\" for the Tower is shallow, and \\"Unity\\" for the Hierophant is simply wishful thinking.<br /><br />The assignment of an archangel for each Major Arcana is very interesting, and will be helpful to those who work with archangels, or want to learn more about them. Following in the footsteps of Kris Waldherr and some other modern tarot designers, this is a great example of what I like to call \\"archetypal assignment tarot.\\" The question is, how well do the archangel choices reflect the standard Major Arcana archetypes?<br /><br />The \\"Unity\\" card (Hierophant), is represented by Archangel Sandalphon. Sandalphon is one of the few archangels who was once a mortal man. This makes sense for the \\"Pope\\" archetype of Major Arcana five. The Pope is a mortal man who is also God, or God's representative on Earth. Sandalphon's responsibilities include prayers, personal ascension and Earth. To me, this seems like a very good fit.<br /><br />The High Priestess is represented by Archangel Haniel. Haniel is feminine, and associated with grace, intuitive development, and finding our divine essence. Again, this seems a perfect fit.<br /><br />After studying the archangels and the Major Arcana cards with which they are assigned, I feel the designers of this deck really did their homework. One problem with archetypal assignment decks is the assignments sometimes feel forced. In Angel Tarot Cards, the assignments make sense, and feel natural.<br /><br />My only real complaint with the Major Arcana in this deck is the loss of the \\"Fool's Journey.\\" I would be fine with the \\"Dreamer's Journey.\\" But here, there is no journey at all. Instead, there are simply twenty-two archangels, each with a beautiful picture, a strong archetypal association and a powerful message. For many, this will be enough.<br /><br />For me, the concept of card zero (Fool or Dreamer) as the star of the show and the representation of each of us on our journey through life is critical to developing a full archetypal and allegorical understanding of tarot.<br /><br />The Minor Arcana of Angel Tarot Cards uses the elements instead of the icons. This is not an unusual switch in modern tarot. The Earth cards have green borders and are illustrated with faeries. The Water cards have dark blue borders and are illustrated with mermaids. The Fire cards have dark red borders and are illustrated with dragons. The Air cards have light blue borders and are illustrated with unicorns.<br /><br />The Court of the Minor Arcana is quite traditional, with ranks of Page, Knight, Queen and King. The interpretations include the possibility of the cards representing people, energies or predictions.<br /><br />I have a problem with mixing archangels with unicorns, mermaids, dragons and faeries, especially since Virtue has made a living of presenting the angels as actual beings rather than mythological beings. Does this mean she thinks unicorns and mermaids are actual beings as well? Many people do, but for me this demotes the archangels to mere mythical beasts.<br /><br />Clearly, Angel Tarot Cards would not be my first choice for a personal or professional tarot deck. However, the artwork and deck quality are stunning. The deck honors tarot tradition in many ways, and will be a great tool for folks who want to learn tarot, or who want to learn about the archangels. It is evident that a great deal of work went into producing this deck. That work has resulting in a strong tarot deck that many people will cherish and enjoy. Angel Tarot Cards is a valuable offering to the tarot community, and may serve to bring new tarot enthusiasts into our community.

0439023513|5.0|Today I know a new truth about my ability to write anything of value. Finally I understand that I write best in survival mode, when I must and about subjects of such great importance to me personally that the need to write becomes an emotional thorn if unmet!<br /><br />I now write about Suzanne Collins' book trilogy, THE HUNGER GAMES. Far from the surface of \\"real\\" and deep within the bowels of contemplation, I cannot escape its implications of dreadful danger to future generations and indeed, to us also.<br /><br />Hidden in the crevices and folds of fantastic science fiction technology, Ms. Collins has locked large, ugly grains of possibility; hinged perhaps un-be-known to her on an absence in the lives of her characters.<br /><br />The finality of her trilogy leaves me breathless in its stark truths and implied hopelessness for humanity. Her suggestion that we are doomed to never learn from our own history beyond a few generations, is reminiscent of how I imagine hell. Thus Ms. Collins's readers face a terrifying conclusion that without altruistic love we are doomed to replicate desperate, self-serving, cruel patterns, sliding helplessly into an unknown yet entirely feasible dark and hopeless future.<br /><br />Yet, just as this conclusion seems likely, Katniss's love for her sister eventually propels her into an ever present and compelling need to protect the innocent, and shines forth in a blaze of glory. Katniss's courageous spirit and her uncanny, unpredictable ability to maintain hope at-all-costs provides Ms. Collins' trilogy with a satisfying, surreal, thought provoking conclusion to this arm-chair adventure.<br /><br />One oversight for me, however, is the author's failure to give her readers a last glimpse of the mockingjay and to possibilities of how its song might impact future generations. I would have enjoyed an ending that included mockingjay songs. Yet even now I linger, rereading her concluding chapter, reflecting on the hope therein for I am notyet ready to tell Katniss Everdeen good bye.

The cast is superb.  Unlike Les Miserables' 25th Anniversary Concert, this production is a full performance, and there is not a weak link in the cast.<br />Ramin Karimloo's Phantom is vocally excellent but packs a lot of emotional punch as well.  Sierra Boggess is lovely as Christine.  And Hadley Fraser's Raoul is noble and handsome -- with a beautiful voice that goes with the beautiful appearance.<br />The updated set and costume are breathtakingly beautiful.  And for a \\"filmed\\" version of a stage version, my, the camerawork is excellent as well.<br />I knew there would be a \\"bonus\\" at the very end, and, man, it sure did not disappoint. I know I'll enjoy it for many, many years to come.

B006Z7Z3S6|5.0|Ti West's \\"The Innkeepers\\" is a tour de force, certainly superior to his debut \\"House of the Devil\\".  It seems to be a bit under appreciated judging by both the comments here and it's critical reception.  I'm not sure why.  It is a bit like peeling an onion but once you do, you don't cry--you scream.  This is psychological terror at it's most refined and disturbing.<br /><br />Sara Paxton plays Claire, a twenty something working at a reputedly haunted hotel just about to close.  Pat Healy plays the distant and sort of strange Luke, a middle aged man who also works the desk and claims to be interested in using EVP to uncover the hotel's infamous ghost, Madelin O Malley.  Madelin was stiffed on her wedding night, killed herself, and her body was stuffed in the trunk of the hotel's basement.<br /><br />For the first half hour of the film, none of this is given what one would call serious treatment.  Claire and Luke goof around, gossip about the famous aged actress played by Kelly McGillis, and it isn't until Claire begins to take the EVP quest seriously that it gets serious.<br /><br />This may lead the viewer to conclude the tragic ending of the film to be a consequence of Claire's overactive imagination or her asthma.  There are a few other things to consider, though.  Why does Lee, McGillis' character, arrive at night just before things get ghostly?  Why, before urging Claire to leave, does Luke say \\"I have something to tell you\\"?  Was his skepticism a mere front for something else he knew?  Why does Lee's facial profile look so much more youthful the last time we see her?<br /><br />Without giving anything away, it seems to me this is an infinitely more complex movie than most seem to realize.  Absolutely recommended for lovers of horror.  Watch carefully.

The last time The Beach Boys attempted to record a group effort like TWGMTR was in 1985 when they were still recovering from the drowning death of Dennis Wilson a couple of years prior. \\"The Beach Boys 1985\\" was a solid but ultimately uneven album (I still really like it though). It was another attempt to bring the troubled and reclusive brother Brian Wilson back into the fold even though the bulk of the burdon of recording this disc fell on the back of Carl Wilson, and cousin Mike Love. The next two discs \\"Still Crusin'\\" of 1987 and \\"Summer in Paradise\\" of 1992 fell flat when the band was under the control of Mike Love and rarely wind up under the blue light of the laser in my disc player. After an amazing decade on the road with his incredible back-up band and a couple of great solo albums plus a critically acclaimed resurrection of the long lost \\"Smile\\" album, Brian was recruited to provide the bulk of the music for \\"That's Why God Made the Radio\\",a 50th anniversary celebration of The Beach Boys long and winding road proving once again that he is a musical genius. Other reviewers on this site have described the tracks on this disc in detail. I won't. All I can say is that by the time the third song from the last \\"From There and Back Again\\", with a lead vocal by the mighty Al Jardine, started playing, I tilted my head back and let the tears of joy run down my cheeks. Carl and Dennis we still miss and love you. SURF'S UP!

B007N5YJYS|4.0|***Spoiler Warning***<br /><br />Strangely, this was my very first Hitchcock film. Strange because I didn't realize it until more than a decade had passed and having knowingly enjoyed many other of his productions over those same years.<br /><br />First screened when I was a little kid and loved for the clever mystery and Hitch's trademark rapier repartee infused screenplay. Additionally, have always had a soft spot for anything related to the 'wrong man' scenario; of being unjustly indicted and chased by misguided authorities. The very first of, if not the precursor to, all later films and programs like '[[ASIN:B00005ATZT The Fugitive]]'.<br /><br />Stars Robert Donat, long a forgotten performer whose incredible skill earned him two Oscar nominations, including a win for Best Actor. Sad that his terrific thespian crafted works are no longer remembered, while most can name every slack-jawed exhibitionist clown amongst a variety of pathetic reality tv shows.<br /><br />Anyway, when this very early Hitchcock movie was written and produced in 1935, the Nazis were just beginning to beat their war drums; the election of The Stunted Mustache two years previous, in 1933, solidifying his twisted base over the German nation made that threat very real. Tensions between the jackboots and the free world were high to say the least. Spies were a true-life genuine threat, the shadow war over tactical and technical information between the Fatherland and UK was already years old before the actual physical call to arms.<br /><br />Thrown onto this volatile powder keg is Donat's character Richard Hannay. A visiting Canadian who finds himself accused of murder. However, it turns out that the victim wasn't an ordinary person, but a freelance intelligence operative who randomly chose him to escape her pursuers. And under his protection, was killed while attempting to expose a plot against Britain. As a loyal crown citizen, Hannay picks up the task of uncovering the truth and simultaneously proving his own innocence. But the authorities don't know that. And worse, the opposition spy network is out to silence him by any means necessary, including framing him as a deranged spree killer.<br /><br />His only clues to escape the hangman's noose and/or anonymous shallow grave: A secret rendezvous, a missing finger, and the strange phrase \\"The 39 Steps\\".<br /><br />Because Hannay is an amateur, his moves are unpredictable, and therefore his only reliable hope of evading the ever growing target on his back. And a chance encounter with Madeleine Carroll's character Pamela, provides the cover he needs. Unfortunately, she  believes the news reports that he is indeed a madman and murderer. Knowing that she'll turn him in, Richard cheekily adopts the role of \\"villain\\", threatening her with death should she attempt to cry out or escape.<br /><br />Unsurprisingly, as the handcuffed pair doggedly work their way past a nest of cloak-n-dagger baddies, she makes a break for freedom. And just as Pamela attempts to reach out to a pair of police officers, she accidentally eavesdrops on a hushed conversation. Stunned, she discovers that Richard was telling the truth all along; that dark forces are indeed at play to silence him before he gets a chance to tell his tale. Vindicated and realizing the stakes of Hannay's mission, the pair race to London in a final bid to prevent technical plans for a silent aircraft engine from leaving Britain.<br /><br />Again, for many years, a wholly unknown Hitch favorite; one that I regularly re-screened during rainy days. I'm grateful to Criterion for putting their technical and artistic skills in digitally remastering this engaging action/adventure/mystery. Simultaneously freeing me from an old beat-to-death VHS tape which engages with some frightening crunchy sounds soon after the introductory credits, thus explaining my ignorance of the director for so many years.<br /><br />Movie Note:<br /><br />- I believe this is the very first time a \\"stealth\\" aircraft is mentioned in the plot of a movie<br /><br />Personal Note:<br /><br />- For some reason whenever a fit of insomnia hits me, and just like the movie, Mr. Memory's musical introduction worms its way into a maddening mental loop of whistling.e a \\"stealth\\" aircraft is mentioned in the plot of a movie<br /><br />Personal Note:<br /><br />- For some reason whenever a fit of insomnia hits me, and just like the movie, Mr. Memory's musical introduction worms its way into a maddening mental loop of whistling.

B0006J28BO|2.0|For about half an hour, this Japanese cartoon seems like a promising and intermittently almost charming adventure. Haru, an unmotivated high school girl who has just a minimal rapport with cats, saves one particular cat by pushing him out of the way of a truck. After he stands on his hind legs and thanks her (in human language), she begins to realize that he is the prince of a most unusual cat population.<br /><br />The plot goes sour when the fearful Haru is kidnapped by a feline mob and whisked away to the royal court of an alternative universe called the Kingdom of Cats. It's a \\"dangerous place\\" whose denizens are generally grotesque and murderous--especially the Cat King, a fat, lazy, gruff, sadistic caricature who is bent on rewarding Haru's good deed by metamorphosing her into a cat and forcing her to marry his strangely absent son.<br /><br />SPOILERS. Ultimately, the only conspicuously likable inhabitants of this court are the prince (who bears not the slightest resemblance to his father except in coloration) and the shy white cat he really wants to marry, both of whose lives Haru had evidently saved when they were in her everyday world. It would be gratifying to observe that her kindness is amply rewarded, but the one who actually rescues Haru is the Baron, an elegant cat statue who befriended her back at the magical Cat Bureau on the outskirts of Tokyo; he suddenly displays supernatural powers that he should have called into play a good deal earlier. Along the way, no fewer than five cats have fallen to their deaths--and that was before the Cat King used a detonator to blow up the high tower that his prospective daughter-in-law and her friends were frantically climbing. Comic relief (?) has also been provided by more than a dozen malicious cats yelping in pain as movable brick walls fell over on them. What age group is this supposed to please?<br /><br />(I know, the old Warner Brothers cartoons were full of comic-book violence, too, but those were usuallyless than 10 minutes long. In terms of content sensitivity, has there been any progress in 60 years, or are we moving in the wrong direction?)<br /><br />This whole silly fantasy seems like a bad dream, and personally, I don't see how it can have much appeal for either cat lovers or cat haters. Notice how practically all the cats in the story are either weak and vulnerable or fat / self-centered / evil / distasteful except a manmade statue--a statue of a cat walking upright and deftly tailored like some trim human lordling, not to say Prince Charming.<br /><br />Yes, occasional moments display a touch of visual beauty or a trace of tenderness, and there is a hint of a moral about appreciating our ordinary lives--or is the film saying, as several characters initially suggest, that saving a cat's life just means asking for trouble? I get it: Risk your life out of compassion for a cat and you risk the unthinkable curse of spending your whole life as a cat--and a cat's consort--unless you can be restored to your humanity by an alluring hybrid creature like the Baron, who is less catlike than human (this is unmistakably a new spin on \\"Beauty and the Beast\\"). So that's why Haru finally loses interest in her high-school crush! Perhaps she has not learned self-sufficiency at all; she has merely learned to believe in fairytale reality and a fairytale hero.<br /><br />I'm not making this up. As the story draws to a close, the clumsy, uncharismatic Haru, looking happy at last, assures her best friend that she no longer cares about that high school boy Machida. Almost immediately, we hear a young woman singing a love song about looking forward to being together again, which continues as the final credits roll. Our 17-year-old heroine has fallen in love with her spiritual guide, in the form of an old statue of an anthropoidal cat that sometimes comes to life. And she had thought that a real cat would be the wrong choice for her to love!<br /><br />Speaking of music: Undeniablyone of the film's assets is Yuji Nomi's symphonic score, which echoes various styles from the tail end of the romantic era (Ravel, Mahler, Prokofiev, and others) as well as later Hollywood composers. But the English-language soundtrack, dubbed in at the Disney studios, is not just annoyingly jivey and marred by lapses in logic (why do we hear that the sun is coming up in a land where it is always noon?). It also reveals that the old Disney tradition of racism is not entirely dead: Tim Curry, as the evil Cat King, seems to be putting on an African-American accent, while Cary Elwes, as the noble Baron, affects a refined British one. On the DVD, one can avoid such distractions by watching the movie in Japanese with English subtitles. Those subtitles, however, duplicate the Disney team's slang-ridden script adaptation (without the minor mistakes and alterations introduced by the dubbing actors); the text is obviously not a close match for the original Japanese. Near the end, we are even shown \\"translations\\" of a few lines that no one is saying at all on the Japanese soundtrack.<br /><br />In short, my advice would be: Skip this one and watch _Whisper of the Heart_, where the same suave Baron character appears in a far more believable and meaningful story--one where cats, sculpted or otherwise, speak our language only when the heroine is dreaming.

0802798225|5.0|In Perfect Chemistry Brittany Ellis is someone who everyone sees as the perfect girl, leading the perfect life. It's a lie, but one she almost obsessively wants everyone to believe. In reality, she's got a sister with a disability, a mother who has her own issues and who is the reason she's so obsessed with appearances and a father who spends most of his time working. Alex Fuentes is a gangbanger, a member of the Latino Blood and someone who no one really wants to mess with. The rumors about him exaggerate things he's done, but, like Brittany, he doesn't mind the rumors that are spread about him. He's in the gang because his father, also in the LB, was killed in front of Alex when he was young, and Alex was encouraged to join to protect his family. He also wants to use the gang connections to find out who killed his father and to get revenge on that person (this is something that the reader could probably guess how it would turn out).<br /><br />When a teacher who insists on seating by alphabetical order forces the two to be chemistry partners for the year, neither is particularly happy about it. While hanging out with his friends, Alex makes a bet about Brittany and has no plans on losing it. But, as the two get  to know and see each other in ways they don't let some of their friends see, they realize that they had a lot wrong about the other. As things with his gang heat up, Alex has some tough decisions to make about his future, though getting out of the gang doesn't really seem to be an option. And Brittany has to decide if appearances really do matter so much that you deny yourself what you really want.<br /><br />I read a lot of YA, but usually it's paranormal. A lot of the contemporary YA I see seems like it would be too much about this guy liked me, then dumped me and just teen things that might be a little young for me, or it goes the opposite direction and is about really depressing topics. After reading Perfect Chemistry, I'd absolutely be willing to givemore contemporary YA books a try. Yes, there's a lot of cussing, some drugs, sex (not much, and I thought it was handled really well) and violence (though I'd bet there's a lot more in reality when it comes to gangs), but what do you expect from a book where one of the main characters is in a gang? The book went back and forth between Alex and Brittany's POVs, which I really like in books and it worked very well in this one. We really get to see what the other is thinking, even if their actions indicate something totally different, and I just loved the main characters. There were plenty of secondary characters, parents (who mostly pissed me off) and friends of both Alex and Brittany, but we don't find out as much about them as we could have (I would've liked to have more of Alex's friend, Isa). While it would've been nice, it didn't hurt the book overall.<br /><br />I know I mentioned that one of the reasons I don't read much contemporary YA is because they might be depressing and you might be saying to yourself, \\"Is a book where both characters are basically living a lie, one's in a gang and the other has to deal with always being perfect a laugh riot?\\" Especially when there are a lot of serious topics in this book like physical and mental disabilities, gangs, etc.? No, but it's not as heavy as you might think. There are moments of humor and Elkeles made the characters seem very real. And, while the author could have gotten overly preachy about any of the issues in this book, she didn't. Other than one little blip in the epilogue (I just thought it was a little far fetched, but nothing that would even slightly ruin anything.), I loved Perfect Chemistry and look forward to reading, not only more of Elkeles' books (I've already ordered a couple, including the next book in this series), but more contemporary YA, period.

She wants to save and protect and she refuses to sit down and let others take care of her problems. This book will surprise you, and I definitely recommend it, especially to young fantasty fans.<br /><br />I'm nervous about reading the sequels, because I heard they have nothing to do with this book. I want to read more about Katsa and Po! But I would love reading about other graced characters. I'd give the other books a shot.

Yeah, its that depressing. There is not mistaking the tone of \\"Rebecca\\", that wasn't the issue. I had issues with the total lack of a cohesive theme.<br /><br />The beginning was slow but I warmed to it quickly. The young, unnamed protagonist, an orphan charge of a wealthy and insufferable woman named Mrs. Van Hopper, falls unexpectedly in love with Max de Winter while attending her mistress in Monte Carlo. She agrees to marry him and is whisked off on a short honeymoon before going back to Max's estate in the West Country, Manderley. Mrs. de Winter tries hard to draw Max into a loving and fulfilling relationship, but she soon learns that Max's first wife Rebecca still haunts the halls with her larger-than-life presence after he accidental drowning a year before. Mrs. Danvers, the housekeeper, seems to hate Mrs. de Winter, and she wonders why her presence as the new wife seems to unwelcome at Manderley. Nothing is what it seems to be, and Mrs. de Winter discovers the secret behind Rebecca and Max's relationship, which could ruin their future as man and wife.<br /><br />There is really no comment on the protagonist because she has absolutely no voice and no real role in the novel. She reminds me of Nick in \\"The Great Gatsby\\"- a narrator who is more of an observer than a central character. She is very meek and deferential and we really don't know much about her. I know it is central to the novel that she is pushed aside and not given anything to do, but I was waiting for her to gain a voice and stand up for herself, and it never really happened. I'm glad there was some hope for her and Max at the end, because for most of the novel, the tone just made everything feel doomed from the start.<br /><br />I had trouble grasping what this novel was reallyabout. Was it a tragic romance, with the young couples' love hindered by the foreboding dead first wife, or was it a crime mystery? It only reminded me of \\"Jane Eyre\\" in tone, not in plot.<br /><br />I gave this novel four stars because of the parts of the novel when Mrs. de Winter believes she will never be able to take the place of Rebecca. I don't think I'd read it again, but I'm glad I gave it a shot. It was well-written, but the plot twist didn't impress me.

B007O3QC8K|5.0|I think this is the best recording that Sir Simon has produced, of those of his that I have heard.<br /><br />It is of particular note that he has produced such a good recording with a piece that is so difficult and widely represented in the catalogue.<br /><br />On the first few occasions I played it, like every idiot listening to a new recording, I paid attention mostly to certain points in the score where I might find certain felicities I savour in other recordings either replicated or improved upon. But as I've listened more, I've come to appreciate the recording on its own terms and I think that it's captured a coherent pulse and appropriately emphasised key points in the score's large terrain. The balance is particularly good and the dynamics are effective (this is a notable achievement for both the BPO and the audio engineers working in the Philharmonie because both have mostly performed poorly on this front since the late 1970s).<br /><br />I love Giulini's VPO DG recording and Karajan's 1966 BPO recording, and Sir Simon's recording possesses several things I value in both those other accounts. He doesn't quite attain Karajan's pulsar-beam drive and concentration, preferring a more expansive and ruminative amble through the score. And he also doesn't quite attain the emphatic and incisive articulation that Giulini does. However, Rattle is able to get the Berliners to play at their full, while preserving the structural tension of the symphony, and his colouring is virtuosic.<br /><br />The fourth movement has passages which sound new and exciting, but few of them. Much of it sounds like Bruckner's Symphony 4 1/2. Obviously scholars have taken something that Bruckner had never felt befitting of performance and turned it into a performing edition. He never suggested people play this material, and therefore one can conclude that it isn't fit for performance - it simply doesn't formally represent late-Bruckner. It isn't finished and God knows what Bruckner wouldhave done to make it performable if he were to have been given another decade to work on it. It mostly sounds rudimentary and banal, which is what it really is - working material made performable by scholars. Imagine what any of the other symphonies would sound like if their much earlier contributing drafts were compiled and performed! Pretty crummy, that's for sure.<br /><br />I would still prefer to have it than not have it, and I think this performing edition should be widely adopted, no matter its limitations.

0061777269|5.0|Consciousness Beyond Life: The science of the near-death experience by Pim van Lommel, M.D., HarperCollins, 2010, 466 ff.<br /><br />Dr van Lommel is a Dutch scientist and physician who worked as a cardiologist in Holland from 1977 to 2003. In 2001, he published in the medical journal `The Lancet' a ground-breaking study on `Near-Death Experience in Survivors of Cardiac Arrest'. This book is the English translation of a book originally published in Dutch in 2007 on the same subject.<br /><br />There are now several books on the market (for example, those by Sabom, Fenwick, Fox) dealing with NDEs and the closely related OBEs. Most of these present anecdotal accounts of visions from patients who undergo some medical trauma (often heart attacks or cardiac surgery). The stories are verifiable in that they agree with the known facts surrounding the event. Most of these books interpret NDEs as indicating connection with some extra-corporeal spiritual state. In some cases, this cosmic energy is regarded as divine. What can be stated with certainty is that NDEs and OBEs indicate an ability of the human mind to undergo experiences that are not explainable by traditional science.<br /><br />There are also books on NDEs by materialist scientists who dismiss any spiritual interpretation of these events and attempt to explain them purely in terms of human physiology (for example, Michael Marsh). Those authors, like Marsh, who attribute such events to a momentary hallucination in patients as they revive are clearly factually mistaken.<br /><br />Van Lommel's book is a convincing account of the study of over one hundred patients during more than twenty years practice who suffered cardiac arrest and were judged to be clinically dead (sometimes for five minutes or more) but who were resuscitated. This study therefore reaches the same conclusion as Michael Sabom, Peter and Elizabeth Fenwick, or Mark Fox. As van Lommel says here, these experiences `cannot be attributed to imagination, psychosis or oxygen deprivation.'<br /><br />NDEs may not prove the existence of God or the afterlife, but they certainly show that there is a dimension of the natural world that requires more than just materialist scientism to explain it. The book concludes with a 23-page Bibliography and a detailed Index.<br /><br />[[ASIN:1907661905 The Afterlife Revealed by Michael Tymn]]<br />[[ASIN:0062517392 Life After Life: The Investigation of a Phenomenon--Survival of Bodily Death by Raymond Moody<br />[[ASIN:0671464469 Recollections of Death: A Medical Investigation by Michael Sabom]]<br /><br />Howard Jones is the author of  The World as Spiritpsychosis or oxygen deprivation.'<br /><br />NDEs may not prove the existence of God or the afterlife, but they certainly show that there is a dimension of the natural world that requires more than just materialist scientism to explain it. 0143037757|5.0|This is a comprehensive, well written study of the transformation of Europe -- both East and West -- after World War II.  Judt points out how stunted Europe's growth was by the thirty years war of 1914-45, and how West Europe finally joined the twentieth century in the great boom period of the 1950s to 1960s.<br /><br />Despite a few lingering illusions -- quickly dispelled in Vietnam, India, Algeria, Africa, and the Suez -- Europe finally shed its imperial ambitions and came to terms with its reduced role in a world dominated by the U.S., U.S.S.R., and China.  The great struggle for Europe was whether it would offer a \\"third way\\" alternative to communism and captialism.  Judt concludes that the socialist ideology dominated west as well as east in the postwar period, but that ultimately a socialist state that cured the perversions of Stalinist communism while avoiding the predations of American capitalism was rejected.  There really is no \\"third way\\" offered by Europe, though Europeans tend to reject what they perceive to be out-of-control individualism and violence of Americans.  The European alternative that emerges at the conclusion of the century is essentially the American system tempered by a larger social safety net and higher taxes.<br /><br />Although Judt takes pride in Europe as the model society, he concedes that the \\"European\\" ideal is fuzzy at best.  What exactly is it other than warmed over American capitalism (and tepid Christianity in a society far more secular than the U.S.), and will even the \\"welfare state\\" programs that distinguish Europe (already being cut back, particularly in Britain) survive the 21st century?  One wonders whether the failure to articulate a true \\"third way\\" will ultimately push Europe into looking more and more like the U.S. as the century progresses.<br /><br />In the end what really defines Europe is a commitment to reject war, imperialism, and genocide as instruments of state policy.  The economic interdependence of the European states does seem to make war completely obsolete, so that has been a great success story.<br /><br />Judt points out that the traumas of World War II left the individual nation states racially homogenous.  This has led to some stability as these homogenous states were free of internal tensions and were self governing, while the rejection of the horrors of conflict caused these different ethnicities and peoples to cooperate to promote peace on the continent.  However, the racial diversity created by guest workers and increasing movements of peoples over borders may test this system, and Europe may yet face the race problems that have plagued American history for the past 400 years.<br /><br />Judt portrays the East as suffering a remarkable stunting of economic growth by virtue of the deficiences of the Soviet system.  The cost of integrating East with West has been staggering, particularly in Germany.  Whether there will be lingering East/West tensions that will tear Europe apart is not a question sufficiently developed by Judt.<br /><br />There is an irritating tendency to portray the U.S. and U.S.S.R. as morally equivalent so far as Europe is concerned.  In the European mind, each superpower was an overbearing master who held Europe back.  This is unfair to the U.S., as conceded by Judt who is highly complimentary of the Marshall Plan and the boom times brought on by more enlightened American policies.  But the resentment felt for the U.S. is a defining European characterisitic, as is the revulsion felt for the high crime rate, predatory capitalism, materialism, deficient social safety net, and religious evangelicalism that Europeans see when they look at the U.S.  Ironically, the Europeans really have not articulated a distinct system, and perhaps what really repels them is that they are so much like the U.S. and have adopted the American approach is so many ways.<br /><br />This is a excellent and comprehensive survey and an attempt to start to put the postwar period into some historical perspective now that over 60 years has passed.

B007I1Q4W2|4.0|With a tiny budget, there's no way to get top acting talent or whiz-bang CGI effects, and the \\"making of\\" special feature underscores how shoe-stringy \\"Exit\\" really was to make.  The low budget shows.  Most of the acting is at best serviceable, and at worst makes the opposite but equally annoying mistakes of being too wooden or too hammy.  Plus, the drawls and accents seemed to slide all over the place.<br /><br />But those limitations aside, \\"Exit\\" gets the \\"for-free\\" stuff mostly right--by which I mean creativity, dedication, enthusiasm, storytelling, originality...the vision part that one either brings to a film or not, and that cannot be bought at any price (I give you \\"Battleship,\\" a thin but expensive reworking of \\"Transformer\\" tropes).  For filmmakers who actually care about their creations, this stuff can be gotten for free and elevate zero-budget movies to remarkable entertainments (I give you [[ASIN:B00001QGUM The Blair Witch Project]], [[ASIN:078401213X Pi]], [[ASIN:B004HY88BI Open Water]], and [[ASIN:B0007N1JC8 Primer]]). All that's required is a love of film and willingness to go a little gonzo.<br /><br />\\"Exit\\" goes a little gonzo.<br /><br />Much of the movie actually is relatively familiar fare, but the setting (postbellum America), the characterizations, the backstories (especially how this zombie plague got rolling in the first place), the use of animation and flashbacks--it all adds up to a unique zombie movie experience. And that counts with me.  I've always loved the zombie sub-genre, and I have been encouraged in recent years to see just how far it can be stretched.  The walking dead have a lot to teach us about bigotry, isolation, consumerism, class warfare, courage, grief, mortality...yeah, the first job of any good zombie movie is to scare you and maybe gross you out, but beyond that, the best ones have something to say.<br /><br />I'm not sure ExACTLY what \\"Exit Humanity\\" was trying to say, but the IMPRESSION it left was of a man who, through incalcuable loss, had joined the dead figuratively (and wished to join them literally) and was brought back to vitality through meaningful projects, human relationships, and natural beauty.  I would say that while the movie was too long by 20 percent and could have dropped a subplot or two, tightening up its storyline to good effect, my patience through the pointless or slow parts was rewarded by a few tense scenes, some tasty revenge, and a satisfying conclusion.  Few low-budget sub-genre movies can boast more than that. I can't recommend \\"Exit\\" in the same breath as [[ASIN:B0044BY9N0 Autumn]] or [[ASIN:B0078YWU6I The Dead]], but at least \\"Exit\\" ASPIRES.

B001IBIHQ4|1.0|Like may reviewers, I feel the need to preface this by saying, yes, yes a million times, I LOVED \\"Band of Brothers.\\"  BOB is a high water mark, a Holy Grail in WW2 films/TV shows.  \\"The Pacific,\\" on the other hand, is not.  Though not a total low point, it's close enough to earn a 1 star.  Major problems are many, including the bad writing.  It's weak.  Very little effort was made to interject actual 1940s era words, phrases and slang, and likewise there is a serious lack of \\"military speak.\\"  I served in the armed forces and, trust me, guys in the services are talking military lingo constantly.  So the dialog came off as unauthentic much of the time.  Likewise, the writers failed to create captivating characters.  Much to my dismay, I found the three lead characters--Robert Leckie, John Basilone and Eugene Sledge--all extremely boring to watch for nearly 7 hours.  Leckie and Sledge come across as morose, sullen and depressed most of the time.  Maybe that's what war does to most men, but it was boring as hell to watch.  Too much of the story also takes place in boring locales--stateside, Australia, a psych ward--wasting time and deflating the excitement built up when the men are actually at war.  The acting also seemed sub-par and lifeless, which didn't help bring any spark to the already flatly written characters.  And Jon Seda seems miscast as the legendary real-life Marine hero John Basilone.  Seda bares little resemblance to the actual Basilone and I just didn't buy Seda as this legendarily tough Marine.  There was also too much sex and nudity, which seemed extremely out of place in this series.  And FAR too many displays of stomach-turning blood, guts and gore.  At times it felt like I was watching a grotesque horror movie.  There was absolutely no need to continually wallow in scene after scenes of rotting and decaying Japanese corpses.  The action/war scenes are okay, but far too little to late to elevate this series marred by extremely poor writing, bad acting and questionable producing choices that fails to even come remotely close to the high standard set by Band of Brothers.  Major thumbs down on \\"The Pacific.\\"

B003Y5HWJU|3.0|Most shows I either love or I hate, such as I loved \\"Band of Brothers\\" and I hated \\"The Pacific.\\"  But season 1 of \\"Boardwalk Empire\\" is SUCH a mixed bag of the good, the bad and the ugly that I truly can't love it or hate it.  It's so middle of the road that all I can say is I think I sort of like it... a little bit... kind of.  There's no doubt the \\"concept\\" is a good one--a show about the lives of a disparate group of people whose lives are linked together by crime, politics and personal dramas in the \\"Roaring 20s.\\"  It's a fascinating time that is ripe for the same HBO-ization storytelling that The Box did so well with in Rome, Deadwood and The Sopranos.  But make no mistake, Boardwalk is nowhere near a good as those three outstanding shows.  There are many problems, the first problem being the writing.  It's weak.  The writers created no great characters is Boardwalk, most of the characters are marginally interesting at best.  The main character,\\"Nucky\\" Thompson, is the greedy, self-serving \\"boss\\" of Atlantic City.  He has no inner-conflict.  He just wants money, sex and power and that's it.  He has it at the start of the season and he has it at the end.  There's no character arc, no character growth, no great dramas or threats for him to contend with.  And thus, he's kind of one-note and boring.  Jimmy, his sort of young protg, is a sullen WWI veteran looking to, I guess, make a name for himself or make big money.  Jimmy's desires and goals are never made clear regarding his ambitions or his intentions with his young wife and son.  He just sort of falls into a life of crime and you never really get to know him or grown to like him.  He's just this sullen, angry young man with no clear goals in life.  Margaret is the lead female in the show and she's also a seemingly ruddlerless character.  A seemingly good young Irish Catholic wife/mother/widow who, at the drop of a hat, becomes a \\"kept woman\\" (which seems way out of character for her on the show).  Besides weak characters, the dialog is also weak, with far too few attempts to use words, phrases and slang common in the 1920s.  Most of the characters talk just like people in the 2000s, so there's no sense of authenticity to the dialog and characters.  This lack of authenticity problem is also present in the set design.  Yes, the sets look beautiful, but maybe too beautiful, too clean, too perfect, because somehow the show's \\"look\\" comes off as too stage-y.  In Deadwood the sets looked real; here they look fake.  The acting is all over the place.  Buscemi (who I generally like) seems miscast in the lead role of Nucky; he lacks the presence, menace and power of his acting \\"counterparts\\" in The Sopranos and Deadwood.  Michael Pitt displays zero range as Jimmy, playing him boring and sullen throughout the entire season.  Kelly Macdonald lends authenticity to her character with her natural Irish accent and spot-on sense of good-girl properness, but the poor writing gives her nowhere to go with her character and she gets boring as the season drags on.  Michael K. Williams, who was SO awesome in \\"The Wire,\\" is absolutely horrible as Chalky White, the \\"boss\\" of the Negro community in A.C.  He makes zero effort to give his character any sense of being a Negro in the 1920s and completely comes off as a modern day African-American person in speech, mannerisms, body language.  His acting is by far the worst on the show.  Surprisingly, the best acting comes from a few of the supporting actors, like Paz de la Huerta, Jack Huston, Gretchen Mol, Emily Meade and Anatol Yusef.  Also the actor who plays Charlie Sheridan, the Irish Crime Boss of Northern Chicago, and the child actress (Lucy Gallina) who plays Margaret's daughter--both of them were outstanding. So overall I give the concept an A, the writing a D, the set designs and costumes a C, the acting a B- (though there are few minor A performances).  I'll also grade the opening credit sequence; I'll give it an F.  The concept, music, style and cinematography of this opening credit sequence are wretchedly bad. B005LAII1C|5.0|When you grow up with a woman who makes teddy bears by hand and sells them for a living, they kind of become that extra member of the family. As a child, there were times when the bears would be in positions or places that weren't where they were thought to have been left. Our theory was that whenever we weren't around, whether it was if we left the house or went to bed or whatever, the bears would get up and throw parties when we weren't around. So any movie that features something like a living teddy bear that can talk probably resonates a bit more on this end than for the average person.<br /><br />\\"Ted\\" pretty much already had that nostalgic factor to it with the concept alone. While Seth MacFarlane doesn't always hit it out of the park with each episode of \\"Family Guy,\\" \\"American Dad,\\" or \\"The Cleveland Show,\\" when he does get it right it's something special; it's hilarious, it's emotional, and its references are spot-on. \\"Ted\\" is MacFarlane at his best. The trademarks he's known for are all in there whether it's the incredible music that is heavily Frank Sinatra inspired, the ridiculous movie and pop culture references, or the humor that always seems to take a flying leap over whatever the accepted standard might be; it's all in the stuffing/inner workings of \\"Ted.\\" The only difference is this is a different medium; a different plane for MacFarlane to bring his offbeat humor and influences into.<br /><br />The spin MacFarlane puts on a familiar story is what makes everything so good. Of course, hearing Patrick Stewart as the narrator is a pretty great way to start any movie. There are a few surprises in \\"Ted\\" that are worth keeping that way. There's this huge emphasis on \\"Flash Gordon\\" that results in some really memorable sequences.<br /><br />By now, you've probably heard the thunder song that John and Ted sing whenever a thunderstorm hits. It's still funny no matter how many times you hear it. The strain that's put on both the relationship between John and Ted and John and Lori's relationship results in the last twenty or so minutes of the movie being purely emotional. So while you'll more than likely remember the movie for the farting scene at the restaurant, the dirty fozzy scene at the grocery store, or the most amazing hotel room brawl involving a teddy bear that you've ever seen, \\"Ted\\" is secretly setting you up for that tight feeling in your chest when that pull string on your heart is tugged a little too hard.<br /><br />\\"Ted\\" is the best thing Mark Wahlberg has been a part of since \\"The Fighter.\\" It also gives the impression that Seth MacFarlane is still in the upswing of his prime and still has a lot to offer to anyone who's a fan of comedy in general. B0086QS7N2|4.0|I became a fan in the summer of '79. They were my favorite band for over 20 years and are still at #2. (Love me some Spock's Beard!) I haven't loved every song they've done. Some albums still resonate more than others. That's just my personal taste. But I have really dug at least something from every period. And that's why I love this cd so much. There are songs & parts of songs that recall just about every style of writing they've ever done, and plenty of new territory as well. Track 1 would be right at home on 'Counterparts' or 'Test for Echo'. Track 2 alternates HEAVY 'Counterparts' with 'Grace Under Pressure' territory. Track 3, the title track, feels to me like their most epic rocker since 'Hemispheres'. Track 4 recalls 'Presto'. Track 5 starts as some of their heaviest stuff ever,  then goes into something reminiscent of 'Power Windows', then back & forth. Then there's track 6, \\"The Halo Effect\\", in which Rush nails the power ballad to the wall! My only wish is this one was longer, but I can always hit repeat. Track 7 is back to the heavier side of 'Counterparts'. Track 8 in parts feels like 'Roll the Bones' and also a little 'Presto'. Track 9, \\"Headlong Flight\\" takes me back to my Senior year of high school and 'Signals'. Track 10 is basically 1:27 worth of Geddy with a string section! Nice. Track 11 goes back to 'Roll the Bones'. And then the final track is so beautiful. A memorable vocal hook sung beautifully, beautiful arpeggios from Alex & Geddy, and the string section! There's 3 or 4 riffs, intentional or not, (I don't care either way 'cause they're awesome), that directly reference other Rush songs, like the intro to \\"Headlong Flight\\" that definitely recalls the intro to \\"Bastille Day\\". The playing is as good as ANYTHING theye've ever done. Sometimes I underestimate Alex's contributions because his sounds are often so thick & layered, and usually a little unconventional. But I really, really dig what I'm hearing from him here. Neil sounds incredibly, incredibly loose. And there are places that contain some of Geddy's most agressive playing ever. I'm not throwing myself up as an expert, but I've learned several of his lines over the years, and there's more than a couple of places here where he sounds so powerful but still in total control. And a big tip to the co-producer Nick R. and the engineers. Wide, powerful, and clear. There's at least four tracks on here I'll be listening to for the rest of my life, just like I do with 2112, Xanadu, La Villa Strangiato, Natural Science,  The Camera Eye, Losing It, Between the Wheels, Emotion Detector, Turn the Page, Hand Over Fist, Bravado, Double Agent, Driven, and many, many more. Peace!<br /><br />(2 weeks later) I can't stop listening. I've tried twice to listen to something else, but I really just wanted to listen to Clockwork Angels again. At home, in the car, at the office. All Clockwork Angels - all the time. Just like the good old days would have been if mp3 players had existed. I know this is just my opinion. There are many other reviews that love it that have wildly different interpretations than mine. I read them and think, \\"It doesn't sound anything like that to me.\\". But it's all good and them some. I haven't dug this deep into a Rush album since Roll The Bones. I haven't dug this deep into any album since '05 with Porcupine Tree's Deadwing. That's how much this record means to me. Going down as one of my all-time favorites. Again, Peace!

B007O3QC8K|4.0|For this CD to attract over a dozen reviews so quickly attests to the importance of any new release by the Berlin Phil.  The orchestra took the Bruckner Ninth on tour, and it's become their habit, it seems, to do a CD as a follow-up almost every year. In concert the main impression one got was of overwhelming power, and although recordings level out how orchestras sound, there's enough here to convince any listener that the Ninth received a powerhouse reading. But there's a cultural dilemma facing conductors now that the Austro-German performance tradition is in its twilight: how does one speak Bruckner as a second language?<br /><br />When Furtwangler, Karajan, Knappertsbusch, Celibidache, and Klemperer were rising, Bruckner was part of the native speech of Austrian musicians, lending such an air of local authenticity that it was often claimed that Bruckner's music didn't travel beyond the Tyrol. It was hard for outsides to grasp his sprawling monumentality without hearing all kinds of mistakes in classical form. Even Furtwangler claimed that only the Seventh was satisfying structurally, and Leonard Bernstein openly disdained the whole canon except for the Ninth.  (This was analogous to the long era of neglect that Mahler went through, always excepting the general agreement that he was a great composer of songs - if only he hadn't embarrassed himself with those leviathan symphonies.)<br /><br />Rattle doesn't speak Bruckner and has had to learn it as a second language, gathering together the elements of his own style - he's certainly not alone in that. What I hear in his concoction is something to admire: solid musicianship, appealing contrasts between drama and lyricism, carefully gauged climaxes built from understated beginnings, and a deft handling of melody. Is that good enough? It depends. This Ninth doesn't strike me as belonging to a category higher than, say, Maazel's Bruckner. (So far as learning a foreign tongue goes, turn to Takashi Asahina, the great Japanese devotee of Bruckner, and you will hear how much heartfelt expression really matters. Asahina made no fewer than three complete Bruckner cycles.) I certainly don't hear the stunning authority of Furtwangler and Karajan. Nothing goes amiss, but there's a difference between building a mansion and building a cathedral. Lost in that difference, Rattle doesn't make the Adagio a stairway to God, which it can be - one remembers that Bruckner was as religious a composer as Bach.<br /><br />As for the completion of the fourth movement, it's the typical cobbled together result of musicologists furrowing their brows for years - there's a shell of Brucknerian gestures with no real musical interest, certainly nothing that could be called real Bruckner. He didn't leave enough finished material, he voluntarily gave up on the finale because of diminished energies, many sketches are lost, and most importantly, Bruckner was a composer of such genius and originality that no group of musicologists has the remotest chance of completing his masterpiece.<br /><br />For me, this Bruckner Ninth isn't a breakthrough for Rattle, but it shows him in a very accomplished mode, with the Berliners following his lead implicitly. 0062183591|5.0|I eagerly awaited my Kindle version of this book, having very much enjoyed the author's other works - especially \\"Predictably Irrational\\". This book is written in a very similar style. Self contained chapters look at different aspects of why we do things the way we do, filled with interesting personal anecdotes and descriptions of experiments conducted.<br /><br />This is a popular science book, in that it does not provide you with all the data, sample sizes, statistical variations and other guff that would serve to confuse many readers, or at least turn interesting anecdotes into dry ones. Instead the author provides a link back to the academic papers he is describing in his endnotes, and I think that worked very well. Here was a book filled with plenty to interest any reader, but a means to verify the claims made by the more academically inclined.<br /><br />The subject of the book is dishonesty and cheating, and Ariely manages to demonstrate some quite counterintuitive facts about our propensity to cheat, showing how effects such as social norms, effects of in-groups and out groups, supervision, and even recollection of moral principles all affect our behaviour. Interestingly he shows that even given the perfect opportunity to cheat to the maximum without consequences, nearly all of us opt to only cheat a little - and he proposes the mechanisms by which we mitigate our behaviour.<br /><br />There are plenty of public policy implications from this work, as well as helpful guides to the reader as to how to control their own nature. I was struck, at times, by how many of these ideas to manage our own moral compass actually were not new at all. Instead, the research presented served to explain why things many people have discovered over the years are effective after all.<br /><br />A real eye opener for me, however, was the demonstration that early intervention for minor misdemeanours was, in fact, much more important than intervention for later ones. Once it was stated, this idea seemed to me to be obvious, and yet I doubt I would have thought of it without the experiments that demonstrated the effect.<br /><br />So all in all a very good and interesting read, filled with plenty of anecdotes, and experiments that seemed to cover a range of walks of life (from students to bankers or politicians) and nationalities (turns out that no nationality stands out when it comes to our propensity to cheat).<br /><br />Thoroughly recommended. This and Ariely's other works should be required reading for anyone who holds to a view that humans always act in the name of their rational self interest. Ariely shows once and for all that we do not.

B000QXDED6|5.0|The story of the battle of thermopylae is rightly one of the most enduring and important last stands in history. Without this battle the whole of European history would be very different. Persia would have destroyed the Greek army and the later victories would not have occurred. It also has all the elements of a heroic tragedy. 300 Spartans hold back the Persians for 3 days at the hot gates (thermoplae in Greek), and end up dying heroically.<br /><br />It is therefore a perfect battle for a good Hollywood film. But this film is more (and less) than a historical telling. It includes quite a lot of historical detail, such as the names of the heros and villains, or the event where the Persian emissaries were pushed down a well, but it also very deliberately plays with that history, by introducing mythical beasts, and stylising the Persians in particular in mystical but very ahistorical ways.<br /><br />The end result is a beautifully wrought imaginary epic, loosely based on actual events. The film was shot on blue screen with studio sets, and this shows. Sparta feels like a sound studio, and the hot gates have an enclosed feel. All this would have felt cheap if it was intended as a historical film, but because it was essentially a fantasy, it all somehow worked, and allowed some beautiful cinematography.<br /><br />However, anyone left with the impression that it really was juts 300 noble Spartans making the last stand should maybe go check the history. True there were 300 Spartans ked by King Leonidas, but they were, in the historical battle, outnumbered by some 400 Thebans and probably even more Helots. Also the enslavement of the majority of the population of Sparta (the Helots) is brushed aside and ignored, giving the impression Sparta was populated Spartans, and not that they were ruling overlords like the Normans in 12th Century England.<br /><br />nevertheless this is a great film. The introduction of mythical elements serve as a warning that this is not a historical depiction, so I don't feel angry about those points as I would if it was being passed off as history. Instead I enjoyed a thoroughly entertaining action film, and would recommend it to anyone who likes action films of any type.
